BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tPREFACE.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThe present work is a modest effort to reproduce approximately, in modern
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmeasures, the venerable epic, Beowulf. _Approximately_, I repeat; for a
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tvery close reproduction of Anglo-Saxon verse would, to a large extent, be
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tprose to a modern ear.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThe Heyne-Socin text and glossary have been closely followed. Occasionally
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\ta deviation has been made, but always for what seemed good and sufficient
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\treason. The translator does not aim to be an editor. Once in a while,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thowever, he has added a conjecture of his own to the emendations quoted
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tfrom the criticisms of other students of the poem.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThis work is addressed to two classes of readers. From both of these alike
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe translator begs sympathy and co-operation. The Anglo-Saxon scholar he
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thopes to please by adhering faithfully to the original. The student of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tEnglish literature he aims to interest by giving him, in modern garb, the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmost ancient epic of our race. This is a bold and venturesome undertaking;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tand yet there must be some students of the Teutonic past willing to follow
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\teven a daring guide, if they may read in modern phrases of the sorrows of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHrothgar, of the prowess of Beowulf, and of the feelings that stirred the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thearts of our forefathers in their primeval homes.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tIn order to please the larger class of readers, a regular cadence has been
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tused, a measure which, while retaining the essential characteristics of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe original, permits the reader to see ahead of him in reading.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tPerhaps every Anglo-Saxon scholar has his own theory as to how Beowulf
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tshould be translated. Some have given us prose versions of what we believe
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tto be a great poem. Is it any reflection on our honored Kemble and Arnold
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tto say that their translations fail to show a layman that Beowulf is
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tjustly called our first _epic_? Of those translators who have used verse,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tseveral have written from what would seem a mistaken point of view. Is it
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tproper, for instance, that the grave and solemn speeches of Beowulf and
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHrothgar be put in ballad measures, tripping lightly and airily along? Or,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tagain, is it fitting that the rough martial music of Anglo-Saxon verse be
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tinterpreted to us in the smooth measures of modern blank verse? Do we hear
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\twhat has been beautifully called "the clanging tread of a warrior in
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmail"?
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[viii]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tOf all English translations of Beowulf, that of Professor Garnett alone
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tgives any adequate idea of the chief characteristics of this great
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tTeutonic epic.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThe measure used in the present translation is believed to be as near a
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\treproduction of the original as modern English affords. The cadences
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tclosely resemble those used by Browning in some of his most striking
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpoems. The four stresses of the Anglo-Saxon verse are retained, and as
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmuch thesis and anacrusis is allowed as is consistent with a regular
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tcadence. Alliteration has been used to a large extent; but it was thought
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthat modern ears would hardly tolerate it on every line. End-rhyme has
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tbeen used occasionally; internal rhyme, sporadically. Both have some
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\twarrant in Anglo-Saxon poetry. (For end-rhyme, see 1_53, 1_54; for
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tinternal rhyme, 2_21, 6_40.)
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tWhat Gummere[1] calls the "rime-giver" has been studiously kept; _viz._,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe first accented syllable in the second half-verse always carries the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\talliteration; and the last accented syllable alliterates only
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tsporadically. Alternate alliteration is occasionally used as in the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\toriginal. (See 7_61, 8_5.)
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tNo two accented syllables have been brought together, except occasionally
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tafter a cæsural pause. (See 2_19 and 12_1.) Or, scientifically speaking,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tSievers's C type has been avoided as not consonant with the plan of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\ttranslation. Several of his types, however, constantly occur; _e.g._ A and
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\ta variant (/ x | / x) (/ x x | / x); B and a variant (x / | x / ) (x x / |
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tx / ); a variant of D (/ x | / x x); E (/ x x | / ). Anacrusis gives
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tfurther variety to the types used in the translation.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThe parallelisms of the original have been faithfully preserved. (_E.g._,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t1_16 and 1_17: "Lord" and "Wielder of Glory"; 1_30, 1_31, 1_32; 2_12 and
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t2_13; 2_27 and 2_28; 3_5 and 3_6.) Occasionally, some loss has been
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tsustained; but, on the other hand, a gain has here and there been made.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThe effort has been made to give a decided flavor of archaism to the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\ttranslation. All words not in keeping with the spirit of the poem have
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tbeen avoided. Again, though many archaic words have been used, there are
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tnone, it is believed, which are not found in standard modern poetry.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[ix]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tWith these preliminary remarks, it will not be amiss to give an outline of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe story of the poem.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_THE STORY._
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Hrothgar, king of the Danes, or Scyldings, builds a great mead-hall, or
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpalace, in which he hopes to feast his liegemen and to give them presents.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThe joy of king and retainers is, however, of short duration. Grendel, the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmonster, is seized with hateful jealousy. He cannot brook the sounds of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tjoyance that reach him down in his fen-dwelling near the hall. Oft and
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tanon he goes to the joyous building, bent on direful mischief. Thane after
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthane is ruthlessly carried off and devoured, while no one is found strong
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tenough and bold enough to cope with the monster. For twelve years he
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpersecutes Hrothgar and his vassals._
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Over sea, a day's voyage off, Beowulf, of the Geats, nephew of Higelac,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tking of the Geats, hears of Grendel's doings and of Hrothgar's misery. He
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tresolves to crush the fell monster and relieve the aged king. With
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tfourteen chosen companions, he sets sail for Dane-land. Reaching that
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tcountry, he soon persuades Hrothgar of his ability to help him. The hours
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthat elapse before night are spent in beer-drinking and conversation. When
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHrothgar's bedtime comes he leaves the hall in charge of Beowulf, telling
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thim that never before has he given to another the absolute wardship of his
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpalace. All retire to rest, Beowulf, as it were, sleeping upon his arms._
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Grendel comes, the great march-stepper, bearing God's anger. He seizes
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tand kills one of the sleeping warriors. Then he advances towards Beowulf.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tA fierce and desperate hand-to-hand struggle ensues. No arms are used,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tboth combatants trusting to strength and hand-grip. Beowulf tears
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGrendel's shoulder from its socket, and the monster retreats to his den,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thowling and yelling with agony and fury. The wound is fatal._
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_The next morning, at early dawn, warriors in numbers flock to the hall
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHeorot, to hear the news. Joy is boundless. Glee runs high. Hrothgar and
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\this retainers are lavish of gratitude and of gifts._
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Grendel's mother, however, comes the next night to avenge his death. She
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tis furious and raging. While Beowulf is sleeping in a room somewhat apart
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[x] from the quarters of the other warriors, she seizes one of Hrothgar's
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tfavorite counsellors, and carries him off and devours him. Beowulf is
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tcalled. Determined to leave Heorot entirely purified, he arms himself, and
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tgoes down to look for the female monster. After traveling through the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\twaters many hours, he meets her near the sea-bottom. She drags him to her
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tden. There he sees Grendel lying dead. After a desperate and almost fatal
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tstruggle with the woman, he slays her, and swims upward in triumph, taking
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\twith him Grendel's head._
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Joy is renewed at Heorot. Congratulations crowd upon the victor. Hrothgar
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tliterally pours treasures into the lap of Beowulf; and it is agreed among
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe vassals of the king that Beowulf will be their next liegelord._
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Beowulf leaves Dane-land. Hrothgar weeps and laments at his departure._
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_When the hero arrives in his own land, Higelac treats him as a
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tdistinguished guest. He is the hero of the hour._
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Beowulf subsequently becomes king of his own people, the Geats. After he
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thas been ruling for fifty years, his own neighborhood is wofully harried
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tby a fire-spewing dragon. Beowulf determines to kill him. In the ensuing
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tstruggle both Beowulf and the dragon are slain. The grief of the Geats is
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tinexpressible. They determine, however, to leave nothing undone to honor
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe memory of their lord. A great funeral-pyre is built, and his body is
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tburnt. Then a memorial-barrow is made, visible from a great distance, that
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tsailors afar may be constantly reminded of the prowess of the national
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thero of Geatland._
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_The poem closes with a glowing tribute to his bravery, his gentleness,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\this goodness of heart, and his generosity._
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       *       *       *       *       *
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tIt is the devout desire of this translator to hasten the day when the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tstory of Beowulf shall be as familiar to English-speaking peoples as that
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tof the Iliad. Beowulf is our first great epic. It is an epitomized history
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tof the life of the Teutonic races. It brings vividly before us our
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tforefathers of pre-Alfredian eras, in their love of war, of sea, and of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tadventure.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tMy special thanks are due to Professors Francis A. March and James A.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHarrison, for advice, sympathy, and assistance.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t                                                        J.L. HALL.

BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGLOSSARY OF PROPER NAMES.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       *       *       *       *       *
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[The figures refer to the divisions of the poem in which the respective
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tnames occur. The large figures refer to fitts, the small, to lines in the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tfitts.]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       *       *       *       *       *
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ælfhere~.--A kinsman of Wiglaf.--36_3.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Æschere~.--Confidential friend of King Hrothgar. Elder brother of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tYrmenlaf. Killed by Grendel.--21_3; 30_89.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Beanstan~.--Father of Breca.--9_26.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Beowulf~.--Son of Scyld, the founder of the dynasty of Scyldings. Father
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tof Healfdene, and grandfather of Hrothgar.--1_18; 2_1.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Beowulf~.--The hero of the poem. Sprung from the stock of Geats, son of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tEcgtheow. Brought up by his maternal grandfather Hrethel, and figuring in
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmanhood as a devoted liegeman of his uncle Higelac. A hero from his youth.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHas the strength of thirty men. Engages in a swimming-match with Breca.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGoes to the help of Hrothgar against the monster Grendel. Vanquishes
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGrendel and his mother. Afterwards becomes king of the Geats. Late in life
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tattempts to kill a fire-spewing dragon, and is slain. Is buried with great
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thonors. His memorial mound.--6_26; 7_2; 7_9; 9_3; 9_8; 12_28; 12_43; 23_1,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tetc.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Breca~.--Beowulf's opponent in the famous swimming-match.--9_8; 9_19;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t9_21; 9_22.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Brondings~.--A people ruled by Breca.--9_23.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Brosinga mene~.--A famous collar once owned by the Brosings.--19_7.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Cain~.--Progenitor of Grendel and other monsters.--2_56; 20_11.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Dæghrefn~.--A warrior of the Hugs, killed by Beowulf.--35_40.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Danes~.--Subjects of Scyld and his descendants, and hence often called
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tScyldings. Other names for them are Victory-Scyldings, Honor-Scyldings,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tArmor-Danes, Bright-Danes, East-Danes, West-Danes, North-Danes,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tSouth-Danes, Ingwins, Hrethmen.--1_1; 2_1; 3_2; 5_14; 7_1, etc.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ecglaf~.--Father of Unferth, who taunts Beowulf.--9_1.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ecgtheow~.--Father of Beowulf, the hero of the poem. A widely-known
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tWægmunding warrior. Marries Hrethel's daughter. After slaying Heatholaf, a
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tWylfing, he flees his country.--7_3; 5_6; 8_4.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ecgwela~.--A king of the Danes before Scyld.--25_60.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[xiv]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Elan~.--Sister of Hrothgar, and probably wife of Ongentheow, king of the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tSwedes.--2_10.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Eagle Cape~.--A promontory in Geat-land, under which took place Beowulf's
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tlast encounter.--41_87.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Eadgils~.--Son of Ohthere and brother of Eanmund.--34_2.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Eanmund~.--Son of Ohthere and brother of Eadgils. The reference to these
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tbrothers is vague, and variously understood. Heyne supposes as follows:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tRaising a revolt against their father, they are obliged to leave Sweden.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThey go to the land of the Geats; with what intention, is not known, but
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tprobably to conquer and plunder. The Geatish king, Heardred, is slain by
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tone of the brothers, probably Eanmund.--36_10; 31_54 to 31_60; 33_66 to
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t34_6.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Eofor~.--A Geatish hero who slays Ongentheow in war, and is rewarded by
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHygelac with the hand of his only daughter.--41_18; 41_48.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Eormenric~.--A Gothic king, from whom Hama took away the famous Brosinga
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmene.--19_9.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Eomær~.--Son of Offa and Thrytho, king and queen of the Angles.--28_69.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Finn~.--King of the North-Frisians and the Jutes. Marries Hildeburg. At
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\this court takes place the horrible slaughter in which the Danish general,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHnæf, fell. Later on, Finn himself is slain by Danish warriors.--17_18;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t17_30; 17_44; 18_4; 18_23.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Fin-land~.--The country to which Beowulf was driven by the currents in
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\this swimming-match.--10_22.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Fitela~.--Son and nephew of King Sigemund, whose praises are sung in
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tXIV.--14_42; 14_53.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Folcwalda~.--Father of Finn.--17_38.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Franks~.--Introduced occasionally in referring to the death of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHigelac.--19_19; 40_21; 40_24.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Frisians~.--A part of them are ruled by Finn. Some of them were engaged
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tin the struggle in which Higelac was slain.--17_20; 17_42; 17_52; 40_21.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Freaware~.--Daughter of King Hrothgar. Married to Ingeld, a Heathobard
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tprince.--29_60; 30_32.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Froda~.--King of the Heathobards, and father of Ingeld.--29_62.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Garmund~.--Father of Offa.--28_71.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Geats, Geatmen~.--The race to which the hero of the poem belongs. Also
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tcalled Weder-Geats, or Weders, War-Geats, Sea-Geats. They are ruled by
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHrethel, Hæthcyn, Higelac, and Beowulf.--4_7; 7_4; 10_45; 11_8; 27_14;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t28_8.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Gepids~.--Named in connection with the Danes and Swedes.--35_34.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Grendel~.--A monster of the race of Cain. Dwells in the fens and moors.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tIs furiously envious when he hears sounds of joy in Hrothgar's palace.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tCauses the king untold agony for years. Is finally conquered by Beowulf,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tand dies of his wound. His hand and arm are hung up in Hrothgar's hall
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHeorot. His head is cut off by Beowulf when he goes down to fight with
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGrendel's mother.--2_50; 3_1; 3_13; 8_19; 11_17; 12_2; 13_27; 15_3.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Guthlaf~.--A Dane of Hnæf's party.--18_24.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Half-Danes~.--Branch of the Danes to which Hnæf belonged.--17_19.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[xv]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Halga~.--Surnamed the Good. Younger brother of Hrothgar.--2_9.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hama~.--Takes the Brosinga mene from Eormenric.--19_7.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hæreth~.--Father of Higelac's queen, Hygd.--28_39; 29_18.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hæthcyn~.--Son of Hrethel and brother of Higelac. Kills his brother
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHerebeald accidentally. Is slain at Ravenswood, fighting against
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tOngentheow.--34_43; 35_23; 40_32.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Helmings~.--The race to which Queen Wealhtheow belonged.--10_63.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Heming~.--A kinsman of Garmund, perhaps nephew.--28_54; 28_70.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hengest~.--A Danish leader. Takes command on the fall of Hnæf.--17_33;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t17_41.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Herebeald~.--Eldest son of Hrethel, the Geatish king, and brother of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHigelac. Killed by his younger brother Hæthcyn.--34_43; 34_47.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Heremod~.--A Danish king of a dynasty before the Scylding line. Was a
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tsource of great sorrow to his people.--14_64; 25_59.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hereric~.--Referred to as uncle of Heardred, but otherwise
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tunknown.--31_60.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hetwars~.--Another name for the Franks.--33_51.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Healfdene~.--Grandson of Scyld and father of Hrothgar. Ruled the Danes
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tlong and well.--2_5; 4_1; 8_14.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Heardred~.--Son of Higelac and Hygd, king and queen of the Geats.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tSucceeds his father, with Beowulf as regent. Is slain by the sons of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tOhthere.--31_56; 33_63; 33_75.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Heathobards~.--Race of Lombards, of which Froda is king. After Froda
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tfalls in battle with the Danes, Ingeld, his son, marries Hrothgar's
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tdaughter, Freaware, in order to heal the feud.--30_1; 30_6.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Heatholaf~.--A Wylfing warrior slain by Beowulf's father.--8_5.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Heathoremes~.--The people on whose shores Breca is cast by the waves
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tduring his contest with Beowulf.--9_21.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Heorogar~.--Elder brother of Hrothgar, and surnamed 'Weoroda Ræswa,'
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tPrince of the Troopers.--2_9; 8_12.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hereward~.--Son of the above.--31_17.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Heort~, ~Heorot~.--The great mead-hall which King Hrothgar builds. It is
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tinvaded by Grendel for twelve years. Finally cleansed by Beowulf, the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGeat. It is called Heort on account of the hart-antlers which decorate
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tit.--2_25; 3_32; 3_52.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hildeburg~.--Wife of Finn, daughter of Hoce, and related to
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHnæf,--probably his sister.--17_21; 18_34.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hnæf~.--Leader of a branch of the Danes called Half-Danes. Killed in the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tstruggle at Finn's castle.--17_19; 17_61.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hondscio~.--One of Beowulf's companions. Killed by Grendel just before
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tBeowulf grappled with that monster.--30_43.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hoce~.--Father of Hildeburg and probably of Hnæf.--17_26.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hrethel~.--King of the Geats, father of Higelac, and grandfather of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tBeowulf.--7_4; 34_39.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hrethla~.--Once used for Hrethel.--7_82.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hrethmen~.--Another name for the Danes.--7_73.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hrethric~.--Son of Hrothgar.--18_65; 27_19.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[xvi]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hreosna-beorh~.--A promontory in Geat-land, near which Ohthere's sons
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmade plundering raids.--35_18.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hrothgar~.--The Danish king who built the hall Heort, but was long unable
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tto enjoy it on account of Grendel's persecutions. Marries Wealhtheow, a
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHelming lady. Has two sons and a daughter. Is a typical Teutonic king,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tlavish of gifts. A devoted liegelord, as his lamentations over slain
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tliegemen prove. Also very appreciative of kindness, as is shown by his
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tloving gratitude to Beowulf.--2_9; 2_12; 4_1; 8_10; 15_1; etc., etc.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hrothmund~.--Son of Hrothgar.--18_65.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hrothulf~.--Probably a son of Halga, younger brother of Hrothgar.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tCertainly on terms of close intimacy in Hrothgar's palace.--16_26; 18_57.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hrunting~.--Unferth's sword, lent to Beowulf.--22_71; 25_9.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hugs~.--A race in alliance with the Franks and Frisians at the time of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHigelac's fall.--35_41.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hun~.--A Frisian warrior, probably general of the Hetwars. Gives Hengest
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\ta beautiful sword.--18_19.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hunferth~.--Sometimes used for Unferth.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hygelac~, ~Higelac~.--King of the Geats, uncle and liegelord of Beowulf,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe hero of the poem.--His second wife is the lovely Hygd, daughter of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHæreth. The son of their union is Heardred. Is slain in a war with the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHugs, Franks, and Frisians combined. Beowulf is regent, and afterwards
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tking of the Geats.--4_6; 5_4; 28_34; 29_9; 29_21; 31_56.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hygd~.--Wife of Higelac, and daughter of Hæreth. There are some
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tindications that she married Beowulf after she became a widow.--28_37.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ingeld~.--Son of the Heathobard king, Froda. Marries Hrothgar's daughter,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tFreaware, in order to reconcile the two peoples.--29_62; 30_32.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ingwins~.--Another name for the Danes.--16_52; 20_69.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Jutes~.--Name sometimes applied to Finn's people.--17_22; 17_38; 18_17.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Lafing~.--Name of a famous sword presented to Hengest by Hun.--18_19.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Merewing~.--A Frankish king, probably engaged in the war in which Higelac
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\twas slain.--40_29.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Nægling~.--Beowulf's sword.--36_76.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Offa~.--King of the Angles, and son of Garmund. Marries the terrible
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThrytho who is so strongly contrasted with Hygd.--28_59; 28_66.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ohthere~.--Son of Ongentheow, king of the Swedes. He is father of Eanmund
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tand Eadgils.--40_35; 40_39.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Onela~.--Brother of Ohthere.--36_15; 40_39.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ongentheow~.--King of Sweden, of the Scylfing dynasty. Married, perhaps,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tElan, daughter of Healfdene.--35_26; 41_16.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Oslaf~.--A Dane of Hnæf's party.--18_24.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ravenswood~.--The forest near which Hæthcyn was slain.--40_31; 40_41.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Scefing~.--Applied (1_4) to Scyld, and meaning 'son of Scef.'
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[xvii]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Scyld~.--Founder of the dynasty to which Hrothgar, his father, and
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tgrandfather belonged. He dies, and his body is put on a vessel, and set
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tadrift. He goes from Daneland just as he had come to it--in a bark.--1_4;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t1_19; 1_27.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Scyldings~.--The descendants of Scyld. They are also called
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHonor-Scyldings, Victory-Scyldings, War-Scyldings, etc. (See 'Danes,'
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tabove.)--2_1; 7_1; 8_1.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Scylfings~.--A Swedish royal line to which Wiglaf belonged.--36_2.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Sigemund~.--Son of Wæls, and uncle and father of Fitela. His struggle
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\twith a dragon is related in connection with Beowulf's deeds of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tprowess.--14_38; 14_47.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Swerting~.--Grandfather of Higelac, and father of Hrethel.--19_11.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Swedes~.--People of Sweden, ruled by the Scylfings.--35_13.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Thrytho~.--Wife of Offa, king of the Angles. Known for her fierce and
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tunwomanly disposition. She is introduced as a contrast to the gentle Hygd,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tqueen of Higelac.--28_42; 28_56.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Unferth~.--Son of Ecglaf, and seemingly a confidential courtier of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHrothgar. Taunts Beowulf for having taken part in the swimming-match.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tLends Beowulf his sword when he goes to look for Grendel's mother. In the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tMS. sometimes written _Hunferth_. 9_1; 18_41.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wæls~.--Father of Sigemund.--14_60.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wægmunding~.--A name occasionally applied to Wiglaf and Beowulf, and
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tperhaps derived from a common ancestor, Wægmund.--36_6; 38_61.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Weders~.--Another name for Geats or Wedergeats.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wayland~.--A fabulous smith mentioned in this poem and in other old
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tTeutonic literature.--7_83.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wendels~.--The people of Wulfgar, Hrothgar's messenger and retainer.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t(Perhaps = Vandals.)--6_30.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wealhtheow~.--Wife of Hrothgar. Her queenly courtesy is well shown in the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpoem.--10_55.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Weohstan~, or ~Wihstan~.--A Wægmunding, and father of Wiglaf.--36_1.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Whale's Ness~.--A prominent promontory, on which Beowulf's mound was
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tbuilt.--38_52; 42_76.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wiglaf~.--Son of Wihstan, and related to Beowulf. He remains faithful to
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tBeowulf in the fatal struggle with the fire-drake. Would rather die than
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tleave his lord in his dire emergency.--36_1; 36_3; 36_28.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wonred~.--Father of Wulf and Eofor.--41_20; 41_26.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wulf~.--Son of Wonred. Engaged in the battle between Higelac's and
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tOngentheow's forces, and had a hand-to-hand fight with Ongentheow himself.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tOngentheow disables him, and is thereupon slain by Eofor.--41_19; 41_29.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wulfgar~.--Lord of the Wendels, and retainer of Hrothgar.--6_18; 6_30.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wylfings~.--A people to whom belonged Heatholaf, who was slain by
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tEcgtheow.--8_6; 8_16.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Yrmenlaf~.--Younger brother of Æschere, the hero whose death grieved
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHrothgar so deeply.--21_4.

BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The famous race of Spear-Danes.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Lo! the Spear-Danes' glory through splendid achievements
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The folk-kings' former fame we have heard of,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          How princes displayed then their prowess-in-battle.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Scyld, their mighty king, in honor of whom they are often called
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tScyldings. He is the great-grandfather of Hrothgar, so prominent in the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpoem.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Oft Scyld the Scefing from scathers in numbers
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t        5 From many a people their mead-benches tore.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Since first he found him friendless and wretched,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The earl had had terror: comfort he got for it,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Waxed 'neath the welkin, world-honor gained,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Till all his neighbors o'er sea were compelled to
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       10 Bow to his bidding and bring him their tribute:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          An excellent atheling! After was borne him
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{A son is born to him, who receives the name of Beowulf--a name afterwards
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmade so famous by the hero of the poem.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          A son and heir, young in his dwelling,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Whom God-Father sent to solace the people.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He had marked the misery malice had caused them,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       15 [1]That reaved of their rulers they wretched had erstwhile[2]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Long been afflicted. The Lord, in requital,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Wielder of Glory, with world-honor blessed him.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Famed was Beowulf, far spread the glory
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Of Scyld's great son in the lands of the Danemen.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[2]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The ideal Teutonic king lavishes gifts on his vassals.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       20 So the carle that is young, by kindnesses rendered
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The friends of his father, with fees in abundance
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Must be able to earn that when age approacheth
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Eager companions aid him requitingly,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          When war assaults him serve him as liegemen:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       25 By praise-worthy actions must honor be got
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          'Mong all of the races. At the hour that was fated
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Scyld dies at the hour appointed by Fate.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Scyld then departed to the All-Father's keeping
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Warlike to wend him; away then they bare him
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To the flood of the current, his fond-loving comrades,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       30 As himself he had bidden, while the friend of the Scyldings
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Word-sway wielded, and the well-lovèd land-prince
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Long did rule them.[3] The ring-stemmèd vessel,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Bark of the atheling, lay there at anchor,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Icy in glimmer and eager for sailing;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{By his own request, his body is laid on a vessel and wafted seaward.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       35 The belovèd leader laid they down there,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Giver of rings, on the breast of the vessel,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The famed by the mainmast. A many of jewels,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Of fretted embossings, from far-lands brought over,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Was placed near at hand then; and heard I not ever
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       40 That a folk ever furnished a float more superbly
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          With weapons of warfare, weeds for the battle,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Bills and burnies; on his bosom sparkled
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Many a jewel that with him must travel
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          On the flush of the flood afar on the current.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       45 And favors no fewer they furnished him soothly,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Excellent folk-gems, than others had given him
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He leaves Daneland on the breast of a bark.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Who when first he was born outward did send him
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Lone on the main, the merest of infants:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          And a gold-fashioned standard they stretched under heaven
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[3]    50 High o'er his head, let the holm-currents bear him,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Seaward consigned him: sad was their spirit,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Their mood very mournful. Men are not able
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{No one knows whither the boat drifted.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Soothly to tell us, they in halls who reside,[4]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Heroes under heaven, to what haven he hied.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [1] For the 'Þæt' of verse 15, Sievers suggests 'Þá' (= which). If
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    this be accepted, the sentence 'He had ... afflicted' will read: _He_
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    (_i.e._ God) _had perceived the malice-caused sorrow which they,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    lordless, had formerly long endured_.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [2] For 'aldor-léase' (15) Gr. suggested 'aldor-ceare': _He perceived
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    their distress, that they formerly had suffered life-sorrow a long
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    while_.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [3] A very difficult passage. 'Áhte' (31) has no object. H. supplies
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    'geweald' from the context; and our translation is based upon this
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    assumption, though it is far from satisfactory. Kl. suggests
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    'lændagas' for 'lange': _And the beloved land-prince enjoyed (had) his
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    transitory days (i.e. lived)_. B. suggests a dislocation; but this is
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    a dangerous doctrine, pushed rather far by that eminent scholar.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [4] The reading of the H.-So. text has been quite closely followed;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    but some eminent scholars read 'séle-rædenne' for 'sele-rædende.' If
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    that be adopted, the passage will read: _Men cannot tell us, indeed,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    the order of Fate, etc._ 'Sele-rædende' has two things to support it:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    (1) v. 1347; (2) it affords a parallel to 'men' in v. 50.

BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tII.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tSCYLD'S SUCCESSORS.--HROTHGAR'S GREAT MEAD-HALL.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Beowulf succeeds his father Scyld}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          In the boroughs then Beowulf, bairn of the Scyldings,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Belovèd land-prince, for long-lasting season
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Was famed mid the folk (his father departed,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The prince from his dwelling), till afterward sprang
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t        5 Great-minded Healfdene; the Danes in his lifetime
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He graciously governed, grim-mooded, agèd.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Healfdene's birth.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Four bairns of his body born in succession
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Woke in the world, war-troopers' leader
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Heorogar, Hrothgar, and Halga the good;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       10 Heard I that Elan was Ongentheow's consort,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He has three sons--one of them, Hrothgar--and a daughter named Elan.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHrothgar becomes a mighty king.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The well-beloved bedmate of the War-Scylfing leader.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then glory in battle to Hrothgar was given,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Waxing of war-fame, that willingly kinsmen
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Obeyed his bidding, till the boys grew to manhood,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       15 A numerous band.  It burned in his spirit
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To urge his folk to found a great building,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          A mead-hall grander than men of the era
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He is eager to build a great hall in which he may feast his retainers}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Ever had heard of, and in it to share
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          With young and old all of the blessings
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       20 The Lord had allowed him, save life and retainers.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then the work I find afar was assigned
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[4]       To many races in middle-earth's regions,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To adorn the great folk-hall. In due time it happened
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Early 'mong men, that 'twas finished entirely,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       25 The greatest of hall-buildings; Heorot he named it
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The hall is completed, and is called Heort, or Heorot.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Who wide-reaching word-sway wielded 'mong earlmen.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          His promise he brake not, rings he lavished,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Treasure at banquet. Towered the hall up
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          High and horn-crested, huge between antlers:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       30 It battle-waves bided, the blasting fire-demon;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Ere long then from hottest hatred must sword-wrath
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Arise for a woman's husband and father.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then the mighty war-spirit[1] endured for a season,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The Monster Grendel is madly envious of the Danemen's joy.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Bore it bitterly, he who bided in darkness,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       35 That light-hearted laughter loud in the building
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Greeted him daily; there was dulcet harp-music,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Clear song of the singer. He said that was able
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{[The course of the story is interrupted by a short reference to some old
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\taccount of the creation.]}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To tell from of old earthmen's beginnings,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          That Father Almighty earth had created,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       40 The winsome wold that the water encircleth,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Set exultingly the sun's and the moon's beams
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To lavish their lustre on land-folk and races,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          And earth He embellished in all her regions
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          With limbs and leaves; life He bestowed too
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       45 On all the kindreds that live under heaven.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The glee of the warriors is overcast by a horrible dread.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          So blessed with abundance, brimming with joyance,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The warriors abided, till a certain one gan to
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Dog them with deeds of direfullest malice,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          A foe in the hall-building: this horrible stranger[2]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       50 Was Grendel entitled, the march-stepper famous
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Who[3] dwelt in the moor-fens, the marsh and the fastness;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The wan-mooded being abode for a season
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[5]       In the land of the giants, when the Lord and Creator
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Had banned him and branded. For that bitter murder,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       55 The killing of Abel, all-ruling Father
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Cain is referred to as a progenitor of Grendel, and of monsters in
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tgeneral.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The kindred of Cain crushed with His vengeance;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          In the feud He rejoiced not, but far away drove him
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          From kindred and kind, that crime to atone for,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Meter of Justice. Thence ill-favored creatures,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       60 Elves and giants, monsters of ocean,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Came into being, and the giants that longtime
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Grappled with God; He gave them requital.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [1] R. and t. B. prefer 'ellor-gæst' to 'ellen-gæst' (86): _Then the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    stranger from afar endured, etc._
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [2] Some authorities would translate '_demon_' instead of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    '_stranger_.'
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [3] Some authorities arrange differently, and render: _Who dwelt in

BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tIII.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGRENDEL THE MURDERER.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Grendel attacks the sleeping heroes}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          When the sun was sunken, he set out to visit
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The lofty hall-building, how the Ring-Danes had used it
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          For beds and benches when the banquet was over.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then he found there reposing many a noble
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t        5 Asleep after supper; sorrow the heroes,[1]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Misery knew not. The monster of evil
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Greedy and cruel tarried but little,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He drags off thirty of them, and devours them}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Fell and frantic, and forced from their slumbers
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Thirty of thanemen; thence he departed
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       10 Leaping and laughing, his lair to return to,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          With surfeit of slaughter sallying homeward.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          In the dusk of the dawning, as the day was just breaking,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Was Grendel's prowess revealed to the warriors:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{A cry of agony goes up, when Grendel's horrible deed is fully realized.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then, his meal-taking finished, a moan was uplifted,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       15 Morning-cry mighty. The man-ruler famous,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The long-worthy atheling, sat very woful,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Suffered great sorrow, sighed for his liegemen,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[6]       When they had seen the track of the hateful pursuer,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The spirit accursèd: too crushing that sorrow,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The monster returns the next night.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       20 Too loathsome and lasting. Not longer he tarried,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          But one night after continued his slaughter
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Shameless and shocking, shrinking but little
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          From malice and murder; they mastered him fully.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He was easy to find then who otherwhere looked for
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       25 A pleasanter place of repose in the lodges,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          A bed in the bowers. Then was brought to his notice
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Told him truly by token apparent
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The hall-thane's hatred: he held himself after
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Further and faster who the foeman did baffle.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       30 [2]So ruled he and strongly strove against justice
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Lone against all men, till empty uptowered
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{King Hrothgar's agony and suspense last twelve years.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The choicest of houses. Long was the season:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Twelve-winters' time torture suffered
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The friend of the Scyldings, every affliction,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       35 Endless agony; hence it after[3] became
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Certainly known to the children of men
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Sadly in measures, that long against Hrothgar
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Grendel struggled:--his grudges he cherished,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Murderous malice, many a winter,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       40 Strife unremitting, and peacefully wished he
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          [4]Life-woe to lift from no liegeman at all of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The men of the Dane-folk, for money to settle,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          No counsellor needed count for a moment
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[7]       On handsome amends at the hands of the murderer;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Grendel is unremitting in his persecutions.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       45 The monster of evil fiercely did harass,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The ill-planning death-shade, both elder and younger,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Trapping and tricking them. He trod every night then
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The mist-covered moor-fens; men do not know where
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Witches and wizards wander and ramble.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       50 So the foe of mankind many of evils
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Grievous injuries, often accomplished,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Horrible hermit; Heort he frequented,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Gem-bedecked palace, when night-shades had fallen
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{God is against the monster.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          (Since God did oppose him, not the throne could he touch,[5]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       55 The light-flashing jewel, love of Him knew not).
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          'Twas a fearful affliction to the friend of the Scyldings
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The king and his council deliberate in vain.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Soul-crushing sorrow. Not seldom in private
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Sat the king in his council; conference held they
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          What the braves should determine 'gainst terrors unlooked for.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{They invoke the aid of their gods.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       60 At the shrines of their idols often they promised
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Gifts and offerings, earnestly prayed they
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The devil from hell would help them to lighten
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Their people's oppression. Such practice they used then,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Hope of the heathen; hell they remembered
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       65 In innermost spirit, God they knew not,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The true God they do not know.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Judge of their actions, All-wielding Ruler,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          No praise could they give the Guardian of Heaven,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The Wielder of Glory. Woe will be his who
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Through furious hatred his spirit shall drive to
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       70 The clutch of the fire, no comfort shall look for,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Wax no wiser; well for the man who,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Living his life-days, his Lord may face
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          And find defence in his Father's embrace!
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    not sorrow, the wretchedness of man, aught of misfortune_.--For
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    'unhælo' (120) R. suggests 'unfælo': _The uncanny creature, greedy and

BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [2] S. rearranges and translates: _So he ruled and struggled unjustly,

BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [3] For 'syððan,' B. suggests 'sárcwidum': _Hence in mournful words it

BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [4] The H.-So. glossary is very inconsistent in referring to this

BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [5] Of this difficult passage the following interpretations among
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    others are given: (1) Though Grendel has frequented Heorot as a demon,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    he could not become ruler of the Danes, on account of his hostility to
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    God. (2) Hrothgar was much grieved that Grendel had not appeared
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    before his throne to receive presents. (3) He was not permitted to
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    devastate the hall, on account of the Creator; _i.e._ God wished to
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    make his visit fatal to him.--Ne ... wisse (169) W. renders: _Nor had
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    he any desire to do so_; 'his' being obj. gen. = danach.

BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tIV.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tBEOWULF GOES TO HROTHGAR'S ASSISTANCE.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Hrothgar sees no way of escape from the persecutions of Grendel.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          So Healfdene's kinsman constantly mused on
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          His long-lasting sorrow; the battle-thane clever
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Was not anywise able evils to 'scape from:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Too crushing the sorrow that came to the people,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t        5 Loathsome and lasting the life-grinding torture,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Beowulf, the Geat, hero of the poem, hears of Hrothgar's sorrow, and
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tresolves to go to his assistance.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Greatest of night-woes. So Higelac's liegeman,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Good amid Geatmen, of Grendel's achievements
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Heard in his home:[1] of heroes then living
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He was stoutest and strongest, sturdy and noble.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       10 He bade them prepare him a bark that was trusty;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He said he the war-king would seek o'er the ocean,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The folk-leader noble, since he needed retainers.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          For the perilous project prudent companions
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Chided him little, though loving him dearly;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       15 They egged the brave atheling, augured him glory.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{With fourteen carefully chosen companions, he sets out for Dane-land.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The excellent knight from the folk of the Geatmen
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Had liegemen selected, likest to prove them
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Trustworthy warriors; with fourteen companions
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The vessel he looked for; a liegeman then showed them,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       20 A sea-crafty man, the bounds of the country.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Fast the days fleeted; the float was a-water,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The craft by the cliff. Clomb to the prow then
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Well-equipped warriors: the wave-currents twisted
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The sea on the sand; soldiers then carried
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       25 On the breast of the vessel bright-shining jewels,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Handsome war-armor; heroes outshoved then,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Warmen the wood-ship, on its wished-for adventure.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[9]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The vessel sails like a bird}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The foamy-necked floater fanned by the breeze,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Likest a bird, glided the waters,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{In twenty four hours they reach the shores of Hrothgar's dominions}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       30 Till twenty and four hours thereafter
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The twist-stemmed vessel had traveled such distance
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          That the sailing-men saw the sloping embankments,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The sea cliffs gleaming, precipitous mountains,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Nesses enormous: they were nearing the limits
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       35 At the end of the ocean.[2] Up thence quickly
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The men of the Weders clomb to the mainland,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Fastened their vessel (battle weeds rattled,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          War burnies clattered), the Wielder they thanked
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          That the ways o'er the waters had waxen so gentle.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{They are hailed by the Danish coast guard}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       40 Then well from the cliff edge the guard of the Scyldings
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Who the sea-cliffs should see to, saw o'er the gangway
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Brave ones bearing beauteous targets,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Armor all ready, anxiously thought he,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Musing and wondering what men were approaching.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       45 High on his horse then Hrothgar's retainer
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Turned him to coastward, mightily brandished
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          His lance in his hands, questioned with boldness.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{His challenge}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          "Who are ye men here, mail-covered warriors
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Clad in your corslets, come thus a-driving
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       50 A high riding ship o'er the shoals of the waters,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          [3]And hither 'neath helmets have hied o'er the ocean?
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[10]      I have been strand-guard, standing as warden,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Lest enemies ever anywise ravage
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Danish dominions with army of war-ships.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       55 More boldly never have warriors ventured
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Hither to come; of kinsmen's approval,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Word-leave of warriors, I ween that ye surely
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He is struck by Beowulf's appearance.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Nothing have known. Never a greater one
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Of earls o'er the earth have _I_ had a sight of
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       60 Than is one of your number, a hero in armor;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          No low-ranking fellow[4] adorned with his weapons,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          But launching them little, unless looks are deceiving,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          And striking appearance. Ere ye pass on your journey
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          As treacherous spies to the land of the Scyldings
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       65 And farther fare, I fully must know now
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          What race ye belong to. Ye far-away dwellers,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Sea-faring sailors, my simple opinion
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Hear ye and hearken: haste is most fitting
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Plainly to tell me what place ye are come from."
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [1] 'From hám' (194) is much disputed. One rendering is: _Beowulf,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    being away from home, heard of Hrothgar's troubles, etc_. Another,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    that adopted by S. and endorsed in the H.-So. notes, is: _B. heard
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    from his neighborhood (neighbors),_ i.e. _in his home, etc_. A third
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    is: _B., being at home, heard this as occurring away from home_. The
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    H.-So. glossary and notes conflict.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [2] 'Eoletes' (224) is marked with a (?) by H.-So.; our rendering
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    simply follows his conjecture.--Other conjectures as to 'eolet' are:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    (1) _voyage_, (2) _toil_, _labor_, (3) _hasty journey_.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [3] The lacuna of the MS at this point has been supplied by various
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    conjectures. The reading adopted by H.-So. has been rendered in the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    above translation. W., like H.-So., makes 'ic' the beginning of a new
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    sentence, but, for 'helmas bæron,' he reads 'hringed stefnan.' This
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    has the advantage of giving a parallel to 'brontne ceol' instead of a
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    kenning for 'go.'--B puts the (?) after 'holmas', and begins a new
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    sentence at the middle of the line. Translate: _What warriors are ye,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    clad in armor, who have thus come bringing the foaming vessel over the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    water way, hither over the seas? For some time on the wall I have been
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    coast guard, etc_. S. endorses most of what B. says, but leaves out
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    'on the wall' in the last sentence. If W.'s 'hringed stefnan' be
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    accepted, change line 51 above to, _A ring-stemmed vessel hither
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    o'ersea_.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [4] 'Seld-guma' (249) is variously rendered: (1) _housecarle_; (2)
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    _home-stayer_; (3) _common man_. Dr. H. Wood suggests _a man-at-arms
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    in another's house_.

BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tV.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tTHE GEATS REACH HEOROT.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Beowulf courteously replies.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The chief of the strangers rendered him answer,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          War-troopers' leader, and word-treasure opened:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{We are Geats.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          "We are sprung from the lineage of the people of Geatland,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          And Higelac's hearth-friends. To heroes unnumbered
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{My father Ecgtheow was well-known in his day.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t        5 My father was known, a noble head-warrior
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Ecgtheow titled; many a winter
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He lived with the people, ere he passed on his journey,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Old from his dwelling; each of the counsellors
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Widely mid world-folk well remembers him.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Our intentions towards King Hrothgar are of the kindest.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       10 We, kindly of spirit, the lord of thy people,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The son of King Healfdene, have come here to visit,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[11]      Folk-troop's defender: be free in thy counsels!
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To the noble one bear we a weighty commission,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The helm of the Danemen; we shall hide, I ween,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Is it true that a monster is slaying Danish heroes?}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       15 Naught of our message. Thou know'st if it happen,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          As we soothly heard say, that some savage despoiler,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Some hidden pursuer, on nights that are murky
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          By deeds very direful 'mid the Danemen exhibits
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Hatred unheard of, horrid destruction
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       20 And the falling of dead. From feelings least selfish
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{I can help your king to free himself from this horrible creature.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          I am able to render counsel to Hrothgar,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          How he, wise and worthy, may worst the destroyer,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          If the anguish of sorrow should ever be lessened,[1]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Comfort come to him, and care-waves grow cooler,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       25 Or ever hereafter he agony suffer
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          And troublous distress, while towereth upward
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The handsomest of houses high on the summit."
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The coast-guard reminds Beowulf that it is easier to say than to do.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Bestriding his stallion, the strand-watchman answered,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The doughty retainer: "The difference surely
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       30 'Twixt words and works, the warlike shield-bearer
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Who judgeth wisely well shall determine.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          This band, I hear, beareth no malice
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{I am satisfied of your good intentions, and shall lead you to the
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpalace.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To the prince of the Scyldings. Pass ye then onward
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          With weapons and armor. I shall lead you in person;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       35 To my war-trusty vassals command I shall issue
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To keep from all injury your excellent vessel,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Your boat shall be well cared for during your stay here.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Your fresh-tarred craft, 'gainst every opposer
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Close by the sea-shore, till the curved-neckèd bark shall
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Waft back again the well-beloved hero
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       40 O'er the way of the water to Weder dominions.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He again compliments Beowulf.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To warrior so great 'twill be granted sure
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          In the storm of strife to stand secure."
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Onward they fared then (the vessel lay quiet,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The broad-bosomed bark was bound by its cable,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[12]   45 Firmly at anchor); the boar-signs glistened[2]
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Bright on the visors vivid with gilding,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Blaze-hardened, brilliant; the boar acted warden.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The heroes hastened, hurried the liegemen,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The land is perhaps rolling.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Descended together, till they saw the great palace,
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       50 The well-fashioned wassail-hall wondrous and gleaming:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Heorot flashes on their view.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          'Mid world-folk and kindreds that was widest reputed
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Of halls under heaven which the hero abode in;
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Its lustre enlightened lands without number.
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then the battle-brave hero showed them the glittering
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       55 Court of the bold ones, that they easily thither
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Might fare on their journey; the aforementioned warrior
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Turning his courser, quoth as he left them:
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The coast-guard, having discharged his duty, bids them God-speed.}
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          "'Tis time I were faring; Father Almighty
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Grant you His grace, and give you to journey
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       60 Safe on your mission! To the sea I will get me
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          'Gainst hostile warriors as warden to stand."
BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
